<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Apollo 13</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the Apollo mission. For the film based upon it, see <a href="/wiki/Apollo_13_(film)" title="Apollo 13 (film)">Apollo 13 (film)</a>. For the Lovell-authored book called Apollo 13, see <a href="/wiki/Lost_Moon" title="Lost Moon">Lost Moon</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">A failed manned mission to land on the Moon</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

<p><b>Apollo 13</b> was the seventh manned mission in the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">Apollo space program</a> and the third intended to <a href="/wiki/Moon_landing" title="Moon landing">land on the Moon</a>. The craft was launched on April 11, 1970 from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center,_Florida" title="Kennedy Space Center, Florida">Kennedy Space Center, Florida</a>, but the lunar landing was aborted after an oxygen tank exploded two days later, crippling the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_command_and_service_module#Service_module_(SM)" title="Apollo command and service module">service module</a> (SM) upon which the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_command_and_service_module#Command_module_(CM)" title="Apollo command and service module">command module</a> (CM) had depended. Despite great hardship caused by limited power, loss of cabin heat, shortage of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Potable_water" title="Potable water">potable water</a>, and the critical need to make <a href="/wiki/Jury_rigging" title="Jury rigging">makeshift repairs</a> to the carbon dioxide removal system, the crew returned safely to <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> on April 17, 1970, six days after launch.
</p><p>The flight passed the <a href="/wiki/Far_side_of_the_Moon" title="Far side of the Moon">far side of the Moon</a> at an altitude of 254 kilometers (137 nautical miles) above the lunar surface, and 400,171 km (248,655 mi) from Earth, a <a href="/wiki/List_of_spaceflight_records#Farthest_humans_from_Earth" title="List of spaceflight records">spaceflight record</a> marking the farthest humans have ever traveled from Earth.  The mission was commanded by <a href="/wiki/Jim_Lovell" title="Jim Lovell">James A. Lovell</a> with <a href="/wiki/Jack_Swigert" title="Jack Swigert">John L. "Jack" Swigert</a> as Command Module Pilot and <a href="/wiki/Fred_Haise" title="Fred Haise">Fred W. Haise</a> as Lunar Module Pilot. Swigert was a late replacement for the original CM pilot <a href="/wiki/Ken_Mattingly" title="Ken Mattingly">Ken Mattingly</a>, who was grounded by the flight surgeon after exposure to <a href="/wiki/Rubella" title="Rubella">German measles</a>.
</p><p>The story of the Apollo 13 mission has been dramatized multiple times, most notably in the 1995 film <i><a href="/wiki/Apollo_13_(film)" title="Apollo 13 (film)">Apollo 13</a></i>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Crew">Crew</span></h2>
<table class="wikitable plainrowheaders">

</th></tr>

</th>

</td></tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Command Module Pilot
</th>

</td></tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Lunar Module Pilot

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prime_and_backup_crew">Prime and backup crew</span></h3>
<p>According to the standard crew rotation in place during the Apollo program, the prime crew for Apollo 13 would have been the backup crew for <a href="/wiki/Apollo_10" title="Apollo 10">Apollo 10</a> with Mercury and Gemini veteran <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/L._Gordon_Cooper" title="L. Gordon Cooper">L. Gordon Cooper</a> in command. That crew was composed of
</p>
<ul><li>Commander <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Cooper" title="Gordon Cooper">L. Gordon Cooper Jr.</a>;</li>
<li>Command Module Pilot <a href="/wiki/Donn_F._Eisele" title="Donn F. Eisele">Donn F. Eisele</a>;</li>
<li>Lunar Module Pilot <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Mitchell" title="Edgar Mitchell">Edgar D. Mitchell</a>.</li></ul>
<p><a href="/wiki/Deke_Slayton" title="Deke Slayton">Deke Slayton</a>, NASA's Director of Flight Crew Operations, never intended to rotate Cooper and Eisele to another mission, as both were out of favor with NASA management for various reasons (Cooper for his lax attitude towards training, and Eisele for incidents aboard <a href="/wiki/Apollo_7" title="Apollo 7">Apollo 7</a> and an extra-marital <a href="/wiki/Affair" title="Affair">affair</a>). He assigned them to the backup crew simply because of a lack of flight-qualified manpower in the Astronaut Office at the time the assignment needed to be made.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slayton_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-slayton-2">[2]</a></sup> Slayton felt Cooper had no more than a very small chance of receiving the Apollo 13 command, <i>if</i> he did an outstanding job with the assignment, which he did not. Despite Eisele's issues with management, Slayton always intended to assign him to a future <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Applications_Program" title="Apollo Applications Program">Apollo Applications Program</a> mission rather than a lunar mission, but this program was eventually cut down to only the <a href="/wiki/Skylab" title="Skylab">Skylab</a> component.
</p><p>Thus, the original assignment Slayton submitted to his superiors for this flight was:
</p>
<ul><li>Commander <a href="/wiki/Alan_Shepard" title="Alan Shepard">Alan B. Shepard Jr.</a>;</li>
<li>Command Module Pilot <a href="/wiki/Stuart_Roosa" title="Stuart Roosa">Stuart A. Roosa</a>;</li>
<li>Lunar Module Pilot <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Mitchell" title="Edgar Mitchell">Edgar D. Mitchell</a>.</li></ul>
<p>For the first time ever, Slayton's recommendation was rejected by management, who felt that Shepard needed more time to train properly for a lunar flight, as he had only recently benefited from experimental surgery to correct an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meniere%27s_disease" title="Meniere's disease">inner ear disorder</a> which had kept him grounded since <a href="/wiki/Mercury-Redstone_3" title="Mercury-Redstone 3">his first Mercury flight</a> in 1961. Thus, Lovell's crew, backup for the historic <a href="/wiki/Apollo_11" title="Apollo 11">Apollo 11</a> mission and therefore slated for <a href="/wiki/Apollo_14" title="Apollo 14">Apollo 14</a>, was swapped with Shepard's crew<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slayton_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-slayton-2">[2]</a></sup> and the original crew selection for the mission became:
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Original_Apollo_13_Prime_Crew_-_GPN-2000-001166.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2350" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/The_Original_Apollo_13_Prime_Crew_-_GPN-2000-001166.jpg/220px-The_Original_Apollo_13_Prime_Crew_-_GPN-2000-001166.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/The_Original_Apollo_13_Prime_Crew_-_GPN-2000-001166.jpg/330px-The_Original_Apollo_13_Prime_Crew_-_GPN-2000-001166.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/The_Original_Apollo_13_Prime_Crew_-_GPN-2000-001166.jpg/440px-The_Original_Apollo_13_Prime_Crew_-_GPN-2000-001166.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Original_Apollo_13_Prime_Crew_-_GPN-2000-001166.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Original crew photo.<br/>Left to right: Lovell, Mattingly, Haise</div></div></div>
<p>Prime crew:
</p>
<table class="wikitable plainrowheaders">
<caption>
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th style="width:150px;"><a href="/wiki/Astronaut_ranks_and_positions" title="Astronaut ranks and positions">Position</a>
</th>
<th colspan="2" scope="col" style="width:200px;">Astronaut
</th></tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Commander
</th>
<td colspan="2"><a href="/wiki/Jim_Lovell" title="Jim Lovell">James A. Lovell Jr.</a>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Command Module Pilot
</th>
<td colspan="2"><a href="/wiki/Ken_Mattingly" title="Ken Mattingly">T. Kenneth Mattingly II</a>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Lunar Module Pilot
</th>
<td colspan="2"><a href="/wiki/Fred_Haise" title="Fred Haise">Fred W. Haise Jr.</a>
</td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p>Backup crew:
</p>
<table class="wikitable plainrowheaders">
<caption>
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th style="width:150px;"><a href="/wiki/Astronaut_ranks_and_positions" title="Astronaut ranks and positions">Position</a>
</th>
<th colspan="2" scope="col" style="width:200px;">Astronaut
</th></tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Commander
</th>
<td colspan="2"><a href="/wiki/John_Young_(astronaut)" title="John Young (astronaut)">John W. Young</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-backup_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-backup-3">[3]</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Command Module Pilot
</th>
<td colspan="2"><a href="/wiki/Jack_Swigert" title="Jack Swigert">John L. "Jack" Swigert</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-backup_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-backup-3">[3]</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Lunar Module Pilot
</th>
<td colspan="2"><a href="/wiki/Charles_Duke" title="Charles Duke">Charles M. Duke Jr.</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-backup_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-backup-3">[3]</a></sup>
</td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p>Ken Mattingly was originally intended as the Command Module Pilot. Seven days before launch, the backup lunar module pilot, Charlie Duke, contracted <a href="/wiki/Rubella" title="Rubella">rubella</a> from one of his children. This exposed both the prime and backup crews, who trained together.  Mattingly was found to be the only one of the other five who had not had rubella as a child and thus was not immune. Three days before launch, at the insistence of the Flight Surgeon, Swigert was moved to the prime crew.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mattingly never contracted rubella and was assigned after the mission as command module pilot to Young's crew, which later flew <a href="/wiki/Apollo_16" title="Apollo 16">Apollo 16</a>, the fifth mission to land on the Moon.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Support_crew">Support crew</span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Vance_D._Brand" title="Vance D. Brand">Vance D. Brand</a>;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jack_R._Lousma" title="Jack R. Lousma">Jack R. Lousma</a>;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Joseph_P._Kerwin" title="Joseph P. Kerwin">Joseph P. Kerwin</a>.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flight_directors">Flight directors</span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Gene_Kranz" title="Gene Kranz">Gene Kranz</a> (lead)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-missionopreport_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-missionopreport-5">[5]</a></sup> – White team;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Glynn_Lunney" title="Glynn Lunney">Glynn Lunney</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-missionopreport_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-missionopreport-5">[5]</a></sup> – Black team;</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milt_Windler" title="Milt Windler">Milt Windler</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-missionopreport_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-missionopreport-5">[5]</a></sup> – Maroon team;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gerald_D._Griffin" title="Gerald D. Griffin">Gerry Griffin</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-missionopreport_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-missionopreport-5">[5]</a></sup> – Gold team.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_insignia">Mission insignia</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_13_Flown_Silver_Robbins_Medallion_(SN-354).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1986" data-file-width="3924" decoding="async" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Apollo_13_Flown_Silver_Robbins_Medallion_%28SN-354%29.jpg/220px-Apollo_13_Flown_Silver_Robbins_Medallion_%28SN-354%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Apollo_13_Flown_Silver_Robbins_Medallion_%28SN-354%29.jpg/330px-Apollo_13_Flown_Silver_Robbins_Medallion_%28SN-354%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Apollo_13_Flown_Silver_Robbins_Medallion_%28SN-354%29.jpg/440px-Apollo_13_Flown_Silver_Robbins_Medallion_%28SN-354%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_13_Flown_Silver_Robbins_Medallion_(SN-354).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Apollo 13 flown silver <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NASA_space-flown_Robbins_medallions_of_the_Apollo_missions" title="NASA space-flown Robbins medallions of the Apollo missions">Robbins medallion</a></div></div></div>
<p>The astronauts' mission insignia was sculpted as a medallion depicting <i>Steeds of Apollo</i> by <a href="/wiki/Lumen_Martin_Winter" title="Lumen Martin Winter">Lumen Martin Winter</a> and was struck by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Franklin_Mint" title="Franklin Mint">Franklin Mint</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_parameters">Mission parameters</span></h2>
<ul><li><b><a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">Mass</a>:</b> <a href="/wiki/Apollo_command_and_service_module" title="Apollo command and service module">CSM</a> <i>Odyssey</i> 63,470 pounds (28,790 kg); <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apollo_lunar_module" title="Apollo lunar module">LM</a> <i>Aquarius</i> 33,490 pounds (15,190 kg);</li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perigee" title="Perigee">Perigee</a>:</b> 99.3 nautical miles (183.9 km);</li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apogee" title="Apogee">Apogee</a></b> (parking orbit): 100.3 nautical miles (185.8 km);</li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inclination" title="Inclination">Inclination</a></b> (Earth departure): 31.817°;</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Orbital_period" title="Orbital period">Period</a>:</b> 88.19 min.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lver_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-lver-7">[7]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Objective">Objective</span></h3>
<p>The Apollo 13 mission was to explore the <a href="/wiki/Fra_Mauro_formation" title="Fra Mauro formation">Fra Mauro formation</a>, or Fra Mauro highlands, named after the 80-kilometer (50 mi) diameter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fra_Mauro_crater" title="Fra Mauro crater">Fra Mauro crater</a> located within it. It is a widespread, hilly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Selenology" title="Selenology">selenological</a> area thought to be composed of ejecta from the impact that formed <a href="/wiki/Mare_Imbrium" title="Mare Imbrium">Mare Imbrium</a>.
</p><p>The next Apollo mission, Apollo 14, eventually made a successful flight to Fra Mauro.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Abort">Abort</span></h3>
<p>April 14, 1970 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UTC" title="UTC">UTC</a> (April 13, 21:07:53 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Central_Standard_Time_(North_America)" title="Central Standard Time (North America)">CST</a>)
</p>
<ul><li><b>Oxygen tank explosion:</b> 03:07:53 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UTC" title="UTC">UTC</a> (55:54:53 Ground Elapsed Time);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-timeline_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-timeline-8">[8]</a></sup> 173,790.5 nmi (321,860 km) from <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rumerman_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rumerman-9">[9]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>CSM power down, LM power up:</b> 05:23 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UTC" title="UTC">UTC</a> (58:10 Ground Elapsed Time)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-timeline_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-timeline-8">[8]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Closest_approach_to_Moon">Closest approach to Moon</span></h3>
<p>April 15, 1970, 00:21:00 UTC; 137 nmi (253.7 km)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Orloff13_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Orloff13-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Splashdown">Splashdown</span></h3>
<p>April 17, 1970, 18:07:41 UTC (142:54:47 Ground Elapsed Time). Crew was on board the <a href="/wiki/USS_Iwo_Jima_(LPH-2)" title="USS Iwo Jima (LPH-2)">USS <i>Iwo Jima</i></a> 45 minutes later.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_highlights">Mission highlights</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Launch_and_translunar_injection">Launch and translunar injection</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_13_liftoff-KSC-70PC-160HR.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1687" data-file-width="1348" decoding="async" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Apollo_13_liftoff-KSC-70PC-160HR.jpg/170px-Apollo_13_liftoff-KSC-70PC-160HR.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Apollo_13_liftoff-KSC-70PC-160HR.jpg/255px-Apollo_13_liftoff-KSC-70PC-160HR.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Apollo_13_liftoff-KSC-70PC-160HR.jpg/340px-Apollo_13_liftoff-KSC-70PC-160HR.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_13_liftoff-KSC-70PC-160HR.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Apollo 13 launches from Kennedy Space Center, April 11, 1970</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2010-06-11_CSM%26LM.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1612" data-file-width="2448" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/2010-06-11_CSM%26LM.jpg/220px-2010-06-11_CSM%26LM.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/2010-06-11_CSM%26LM.jpg/330px-2010-06-11_CSM%26LM.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/2010-06-11_CSM%26LM.jpg/440px-2010-06-11_CSM%26LM.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2010-06-11_CSM%26LM.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Apollo 13 spacecraft configuration en route to the Moon</div></div></div>
<p>The mission was launched at the planned time, 02:13:00 PM <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Standard_Time_(North_America)" title="Eastern Standard Time (North America)">EST</a> (19:13:00 UTC) on April 11. An anomaly occurred when the second-stage, center (inboard) engine shut down about two minutes early. The four outboard engines and the third-stage engine burned longer to compensate, and the vehicle achieved very close to the planned circular 100 nautical miles (190 km) <a href="/wiki/Parking_orbit" title="Parking orbit">parking orbit</a>, followed by a normal translunar injection about two hours later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lver_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-lver-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> The engine shutdown was determined to be caused by severe <a href="/wiki/Pogo_oscillation" title="Pogo oscillation">pogo oscillations</a> measured at a strength of 68 <i>g</i> and a frequency of 16 hertz, flexing the thrust frame by 3 inches (76 mm). The vehicle's guidance system shut the engine down in response to sensed thrust chamber pressure fluctuations. Pogo oscillations had been seen on previous Titan rockets, and also on the Saturn V during <a href="/wiki/Apollo_6" title="Apollo 6">Apollo 6</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> but on Apollo 13, they were amplified by an unexpected interaction with turbopump <a href="/wiki/Cavitation" title="Cavitation">cavitation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Later missions implemented anti-pogo modifications that had been under development. These included addition of a helium-gas reservoir to the center engine <a href="/wiki/Liquid_oxygen" title="Liquid oxygen">liquid oxygen</a> line to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Damping" title="Damping">damp</a> pressure oscillations, an automatic cutoff as a backup, and simplification of the propellant valves of all five second-stage engines.
</p><p>The crew performed the separation and transposition maneuver to dock the CM <i>Odyssey</i> to the LM <i>Aquarius</i>, and pulled away from the spent third stage, which ground controllers then sent on a course to impact the Moon in range of a seismometer placed on the surface by <a href="/wiki/Apollo_12" title="Apollo 12">Apollo 12</a>. They then settled in for the three-day trip to Fra Mauro.
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Accident">Accident</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mission_Operations_Control_Room_during_Apollo_13.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2172" data-file-width="2954" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Mission_Operations_Control_Room_during_Apollo_13.jpg/220px-Mission_Operations_Control_Room_during_Apollo_13.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Mission_Operations_Control_Room_during_Apollo_13.jpg/330px-Mission_Operations_Control_Room_during_Apollo_13.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Mission_Operations_Control_Room_during_Apollo_13.jpg/440px-Mission_Operations_Control_Room_during_Apollo_13.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mission_Operations_Control_Room_during_Apollo_13.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mission Operations Control Room during Apollo 13's fourth television transmission, on the evening of April 13, 1970. Lunar Module Pilot Fred Haise Jr. is seen on the screen.</div></div></div>
<p>Approaching 56 hours into the mission, Apollo 13 was approximately 205,000 miles (330,000 km) from Earth en route to the Moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gatland_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gatland-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Approximately six and a half minutes after the end of a live TV broadcast from the spacecraft, Haise was in the process of closing out the LM, while Lovell was stowing the TV camera. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson_Space_Center" title="Lyndon B. Johnson Space Center">Houston</a> flight controllers asked Swigert to turn on the hydrogen and oxygen tank stirring fans in the service module, which were designed to destratify the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryogenic" title="Cryogenic">cryogenic</a> contents and increase the accuracy of their quantity readings. Two minutes later, the astronauts heard a "pretty large bang", accompanied by fluctuations in electrical power and the firing of the attitude control thrusters;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-timeline_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-timeline-8">[8]</a></sup> the crew initially thought that a <a href="/wiki/Meteoroid" title="Meteoroid">meteoroid</a> might have struck the lunar module. Communications and telemetry to Earth were lost for 1.8 seconds, until the system automatically corrected by switching the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_gain_antenna" title="High gain antenna">high-gain</a> <a href="/wiki/Unified_S-band" title="Unified S-band">S-band</a> antenna, used for translunar communications, from narrow-beam to wide-beam mode.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="mbox-small noprint" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">
<tbody><tr>

<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">
<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:Apollo13-wehaveaproblem.ogg" title="File:Apollo13-wehaveaproblem.ogg">Houston, we've had a problem.</a></div>
<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio class="kskin" controls="" data-durationhint="178.66047619048" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Apollo13-wehaveaproblem.ogg" data-startoffset="0" id="mwe_player_0" preload="none" style="width:220px"><source data-bandwidth="163178" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (163 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6f/Apollo13-wehaveaproblem.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="233944" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-title="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/6/6f/Apollo13-wehaveaproblem.ogg/Apollo13-wehaveaproblem.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg"/><track data-dir="ltr" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="TimedText:Apollo13-wehaveaproblem.ogg.en.srt" kind="subtitles" label="English (en) subtitles" src="https://commons.wikimedia.org/w/index.php?title=TimedText:Apollo13-wehaveaproblem.ogg.en.srt&amp;action=raw&amp;ctype=text%2Fx-srt" srclang="en" type="text/x-srt"/><track data-dir="ltr" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="TimedText:Apollo13-wehaveaproblem.ogg.ru.srt" kind="subtitles" label="русский (ru) subtitles" src="https://commons.wikimedia.org/w/index.php?title=TimedText:Apollo13-wehaveaproblem.ogg.ru.srt&amp;action=raw&amp;ctype=text%2Fx-srt" srclang="ru" type="text/x-srt"/></audio></div></div>
<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">Swigert and Lovell reporting the incident on April 14, 1970 [2:59] "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Houston,_we%27ve_had_a_problem" title="Houston, we've had a problem">Houston, we've had a problem</a>"</div></div></td></tr>
<tr><td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr/><span class="selfreference"><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></span></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Immediately after the bang, Swigert reported a "problem", which Lovell repeated and clarified as a "main B bus undervolt", a temporary loss of operating voltage on the second of the spacecraft's main electrical circuits. Oxygen tank 2 immediately read quantity zero. About three minutes later, the number 1 and number 3 fuel cells failed. Lovell reported seeing out the window that the craft was venting "a gas of some sort" into space. The number 1 oxygen tank quantity gradually reduced to zero over the next 130 minutes, entirely depleting the SM's oxygen supply.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-report_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-report-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because the fuel cells generated the command and service module's electrical power by combining hydrogen and oxygen into water, when oxygen tank 1 ran dry, the remaining fuel cell finally shut down, leaving the craft on the command module's limited-duration battery power and water. The crew was forced to shut down the CM completely to save this for reentry, and to power up the LM to use as a "lifeboat".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> This situation had been suggested during an earlier training simulation, but had not been considered a likely scenario.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Without the LM, the accident would certainly have been fatal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:802px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_13_timeline.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="400" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Apollo_13_timeline.svg/800px-Apollo_13_timeline.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Apollo_13_timeline.svg/1200px-Apollo_13_timeline.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Apollo_13_timeline.svg/1600px-Apollo_13_timeline.svg.png 2x" width="800"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_13_timeline.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Circumlunar_trajectory" title="Circumlunar trajectory">circumlunar trajectory</a> followed by Apollo 13, drawn to scale; the accident occurred about 5​<span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>2</sub></span> hours from entry into the Moon's sphere of gravitational influence</div></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crew_survival_and_return_journey">Crew survival and return journey</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Direct_Abort_Trajectory_-_Lunar_Landing_Symposium,_MSC_Jun66.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="885" data-file-width="1136" decoding="async" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Direct_Abort_Trajectory_-_Lunar_Landing_Symposium%2C_MSC_Jun66.jpg/220px-Direct_Abort_Trajectory_-_Lunar_Landing_Symposium%2C_MSC_Jun66.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Direct_Abort_Trajectory_-_Lunar_Landing_Symposium%2C_MSC_Jun66.jpg/330px-Direct_Abort_Trajectory_-_Lunar_Landing_Symposium%2C_MSC_Jun66.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Direct_Abort_Trajectory_-_Lunar_Landing_Symposium%2C_MSC_Jun66.jpg/440px-Direct_Abort_Trajectory_-_Lunar_Landing_Symposium%2C_MSC_Jun66.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Direct_Abort_Trajectory_-_Lunar_Landing_Symposium,_MSC_Jun66.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Direct Abort return, depicted in a 1966 planning report. The trajectory shown is at a point much earlier and farther away from the Moon than where the Apollo 13 accident happened.</div></div></div>
<p>The damage to the service module made safe return from a lunar landing impossible, so Lead Flight Director <a href="/wiki/Gene_Kranz" title="Gene Kranz">Gene Kranz</a> ordered an abort of the mission. The existing abort plans, first drawn up in 1966, were evaluated; the quickest was a Direct Abort trajectory, which required using the service propulsion system (SPS) engine to achieve a 6,079-foot-per-second (1,853 m/s) <a href="/wiki/Delta-v" title="Delta-v">delta-<i>v</i></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-missionopreport_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-missionopreport-5">[5]</a></sup><sup>p. III-14</sup> Although a successful SPS firing at 60 hours ground elapsed time (GET) would land the crew one day earlier (at 118 hours GET, or 58 hours later), the large delta-<i>v</i> was possible only if the LM were jettisoned first,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-missionopreport_5-5"><a href="#cite_note-missionopreport-5">[5]</a></sup><sup>p. II-1</sup> and since crew survival depended on the LM's presence during the coast back to Earth, that option was "out of the question."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-missionopreport_5-6"><a href="#cite_note-missionopreport-5">[5]</a></sup><sup>p. III-17</sup> An alternative would have been to burn the SPS fuel to depletion, then jettison the service module and make a second burn with the LM <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Descent_Propulsion_System" title="Descent Propulsion System">Descent Propulsion System</a> (DPS) engine. It was desired to keep the service module attached for as long as possible because of the thermal protection it afforded the command module's heat shield. Apollo 13 was close to entering the lunar <a href="/wiki/Sphere_of_influence_(astrodynamics)" title="Sphere of influence (astrodynamics)">sphere of gravitational influence</a> (at 61 hours GET), which was the break-even point between direct and circumlunar aborts, and the latter allowed more time for evaluation and planning before a major rocket burn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-missionopreport_5-7"><a href="#cite_note-missionopreport-5">[5]</a></sup><sup>p. B-5</sup> There also was concern about "the structural integrity of the Service Module,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-missionopreport_5-8"><a href="#cite_note-missionopreport-5">[5]</a></sup><sup>p. III‑23</sup> so mission planners were instructed that the SPS engine would not be used "except as a last-ditch effort."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-missionopreport_5-9"><a href="#cite_note-missionopreport-5">[5]</a></sup><sup>p. III-14</sup>
</p><p>For these reasons, Kranz chose the alternative circumlunar option, using the Moon's gravity to return the ship to Earth. Apollo 13 had left its initial <a href="/wiki/Free-return_trajectory" title="Free-return trajectory">free-return trajectory</a> earlier in the mission, as required for the lunar landing at Fra Mauro. Therefore, the first order of business was to re-establish the free-return trajectory with a 30.7-second burn of the DPS. The descent engine was used again two hours after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pericynthion" title="Pericynthion">pericynthion</a>, the closest approach to the Moon ("PC+2 burn"), to speed the return to Earth by 10 hours and move the landing spot from the Indian Ocean to the Pacific Ocean. A more aggressive burn could have been performed at PC+2 by first jettisoning the service module, returning the crew in about the same amount of time as a direct abort,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-missionopreport_5-10"><a href="#cite_note-missionopreport-5">[5]</a></sup><sup>p. III-20</sup> but this was deemed unnecessary given the rates at which consumables were being used. The 4-minute, 24-second burn was so accurate that only two more small course corrections were subsequently needed.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo13_apparatus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3888" data-file-width="3888" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Apollo13_apparatus.jpg/220px-Apollo13_apparatus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Apollo13_apparatus.jpg/330px-Apollo13_apparatus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Apollo13_apparatus.jpg/440px-Apollo13_apparatus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo13_apparatus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Astronaut John L. Swigert, at right, with the "mailbox" rig improvised to adapt the command module's square <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> scrubber cartridges to fit the lunar module, which took a round cartridge</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_13_Mailbox_at_Mission_Control.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2611" data-file-width="4012" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Apollo_13_Mailbox_at_Mission_Control.jpg/220px-Apollo_13_Mailbox_at_Mission_Control.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Apollo_13_Mailbox_at_Mission_Control.jpg/330px-Apollo_13_Mailbox_at_Mission_Control.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Apollo_13_Mailbox_at_Mission_Control.jpg/440px-Apollo_13_Mailbox_at_Mission_Control.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_13_Mailbox_at_Mission_Control.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The "mailbox" at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mission_Control#NASA_Mission_Control_Center" title="Mission Control">Mission Control</a> during the Apollo 13 mission</div></div></div>
<p>Considerable ingenuity under extreme pressure was required from the crew, <a href="/wiki/Flight_controller" title="Flight controller">flight controllers</a>, and support personnel for the safe return. The developing drama was shown on television.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Because electrical power was severely limited, no more live TV broadcasts were made; TV commentators used models and animated footage as illustrations. Low power levels made even voice communications difficult.
</p><p>The lunar module consumables were intended to sustain two people for a day and a half, not three people for four days. Oxygen was the least critical consumable because the LM carried enough to repressurize the LM after each surface <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extra-vehicular_activity" title="Extra-vehicular activity">EVA</a>. Unlike the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Command_and_service_module" title="Command and service module">command and service module</a> (CSM), which was powered by fuel cells that produced water as a byproduct, the LM was powered by <a href="/wiki/Silver-oxide_battery" title="Silver-oxide battery">silver-zinc batteries</a>, so electrical power and water (used for equipment cooling as well as drinking) were critical consumables. To keep the LM life-support and communication systems operational until reentry, the LM was powered down to the lowest levels possible. In particular, the LM's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abort_Guidance_System" title="Abort Guidance System">Abort Guidance System</a> was used for most of the coast back to Earth instead of the primary guidance system, as it used less power and water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-missionopreport_5-11"><a href="#cite_note-missionopreport-5">[5]</a></sup><sup>pp. III‑17,33,40</sup>
</p><p>Availability of <a href="/wiki/Lithium_hydroxide" title="Lithium hydroxide">lithium hydroxide</a> (LiOH) for <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_scrubber" title="Carbon dioxide scrubber">removing carbon dioxide</a> presented a serious problem. The LM's internal stock of LiOH canisters was not sufficient to support the crew until return, and the remainder was stored in the descent stage, out of reach. The CM had an adequate supply of canisters, but these were incompatible with the LM. Engineers on the ground improvised a way to join the cube-shaped CM canisters to the LM's cylindrical canister-sockets by drawing air through them with a suit return hose. NASA engineers referred to the improvised device as "the mailbox".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another problem to be solved for a safe return was accomplishing a complete power-up from scratch of the completely shut-down command module, something never intended to be done in-flight. Flight controller <a href="/wiki/John_Aaron" title="John Aaron">John Aaron</a>, with the support of grounded astronaut Mattingly and many engineers and designers, had to invent a new procedure to do this with the ship's limited power supply and time factor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> This was further complicated by the fact that the reduced power levels in the LM caused internal temperatures to drop to as low as 4 °C (39 °F). The unpowered CM got so cold that water began to <a href="/wiki/Condensation" title="Condensation">condense</a> on solid surfaces, causing concern that this might short out electrical systems when it was reactivated. This turned out not to be a problem, partly because of the extensive electrical insulation improvements instituted after the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_1" title="Apollo 1">Apollo 1</a> fire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>The last problem to be solved was how to separate the lunar module a safe distance away from the command module just before reentry. The normal procedure was to use the service module's <a href="/wiki/Reaction_control_system" title="Reaction control system">reaction control system</a> (RCS) to pull the CSM away after releasing the LM along with the command module's docking ring, but this RCS was inoperative because of the power failure, and the useless SM would be released before the LM. To solve the problem, Grumman called on the engineering expertise of the University of Toronto. A team of six UT engineers, led by senior scientist <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Etkin" title="Bernard Etkin">Bernard Etkin</a>, was formed to solve the problem within a day. The team concluded that pressurizing the tunnel connecting the lunar module to the command module just before separation would provide the force necessary to push the two modules a safe distance away from each other just prior to reentry. The team had 6 hours to compute the pressure required, using <a href="/wiki/Slide_rule" title="Slide rule">slide rules</a>. They needed an accurate calculation, as too high a pressure might damage the hatch and its seal, causing the astronauts to burn up; too low a pressure would not provide enough separation distance of the LM. Grumman relayed their calculation to NASA, and from there in turn to the astronauts, who used it successfully.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G&amp;M_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-G&amp;M-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reentry_and_splashdown">Reentry and splashdown</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo13_splashdown.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Apollo13_splashdown.jpg/220px-Apollo13_splashdown.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Apollo13_splashdown.jpg/330px-Apollo13_splashdown.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Apollo13_splashdown.jpg/440px-Apollo13_splashdown.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo13_splashdown.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Apollo 13 splashes down in the South Pacific on April 17, 1970</div></div></div>
<p>As Apollo 13 neared Earth, the crew first jettisoned the service module, using the LM's <a href="/wiki/Reaction_control_system" title="Reaction control system">reaction control system</a> to pull themselves a safe distance from it, instead of the normal procedure which used automatic firing of the SM's RCS. They photographed it for later analysis of the accident's cause. It was then that the crew were surprised to see for the first time that the entire Sector 4 panel had been blown off. According to the analysts, these pictures also showed the antenna damage and possibly an upward tilt to the fuel cell shelf above the oxygen tank compartment.
</p><p>Finally, the crew jettisoned the lunar module <i>Aquarius</i> using the above procedure worked out at the University of Toronto, leaving the command module <i>Odyssey</i> to begin its lone reentry through the atmosphere. The reentry on a lunar mission normally was accompanied by about four minutes of typical communications blackout caused by ionization of the air around the command module. The blackout in Apollo 13's reentry lasted six minutes, which was 87 seconds longer than had been expected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The possibility of heat-shield damage from the <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> tank rupture heightened the tension of the blackout period.
</p><p><i>Odyssey</i> regained radio contact and splashed down safely in the South Pacific Ocean, <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Apollo_13&amp;params=21_38_24_S_165_21_42_W_type:event&amp;title=Apollo+13+splashdown"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">21°38′24″S</span> <span class="longitude">165°21′42″W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">21.64000°S 165.36167°W</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">-21.64000; -165.36167</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">Apollo 13 splashdown</span>)</span></span></span></a></span>, southeast of <a href="/wiki/American_Samoa" title="American Samoa">American Samoa</a> and 6.5 km (3.5 nmi) from the recovery ship, <a href="/wiki/USS_Iwo_Jima_(LPH-2)" title="USS Iwo Jima (LPH-2)">USS <i>Iwo Jima</i></a>.
The crew was in good condition except for Haise, who was suffering from a serious <a href="/wiki/Urinary_tract_infection" title="Urinary tract infection">urinary tract infection</a> because of insufficient water intake. To avoid altering the trajectory of the spacecraft, the crew had been instructed to temporarily stop urine dumps, which forced them to invent ways of storing all urine for the rest of the flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>The lunar module and service module reentered the atmosphere over the South Pacific between the islands of Fiji and New Zealand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>

</p>

</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;"><div style="margin:24.5px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_13_Service_Module_and_Lunar_Module_as_entering_Earth%27s_atmosphere_Download.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2808" data-file-width="3100" decoding="async" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Apollo_13_Service_Module_and_Lunar_Module_as_entering_Earth%27s_atmosphere_Download.jpg/200px-Apollo_13_Service_Module_and_Lunar_Module_as_entering_Earth%27s_atmosphere_Download.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Apollo_13_Service_Module_and_Lunar_Module_as_entering_Earth%27s_atmosphere_Download.jpg/300px-Apollo_13_Service_Module_and_Lunar_Module_as_entering_Earth%27s_atmosphere_Download.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Apollo_13_Service_Module_and_Lunar_Module_as_entering_Earth%27s_atmosphere_Download.jpg/400px-Apollo_13_Service_Module_and_Lunar_Module_as_entering_Earth%27s_atmosphere_Download.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div>

</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Analysis_and_response">Analysis and response</span></h2>
<p>NASA Administrator <a href="/wiki/Thomas_O._Paine" title="Thomas O. Paine">Thomas Paine</a> and Deputy Administrator <a href="/wiki/George_Low" title="George Low">George Low</a> sent a memorandum to NASA <a href="/wiki/Langley_Research_Center" title="Langley Research Center">Langley Research Center</a> Director <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Cortright" title="Edgar Cortright">Edgar M. Cortright</a> on April 17, 1970, (date of spacecraft splashdown) advising him of his appointment as chairman of an <b>Apollo 13 Review Board</b> to investigate the cause of the accident.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Review_board">Review board</span></h3>
<p>The second memorandum to Cortright from Paine and Low on April 21 established the board as follows:
</p>
<dl><dd><b>Members:</b><ul><li>Robert F. Allnutt (Assistant to the Administrator, NASA Hqs.);</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Neil_Armstrong" title="Neil Armstrong">Neil Armstrong</a> (Astronaut, Manned Spacecraft Center);</li>
<li>Dr. John F. Clark (Director, Goddard Space Flight Center);</li>
<li>Brig. General Walter R. Hedrick Jr. (Director of Space, DCS/RED, Hqs., USAF);</li>
<li>Vincent L. Johnson (Deputy Associate Administrator-Engineering, Office of Space Science and Applications);</li>
<li>Milton Klein (Manager, AEC-NASA Space Nuclear Propulsion Office);</li>
<li>Dr. Hans M. Mark (Director, Ames Research Center).</li></ul><b>Counsel:</b><ul><li>George Malley (Chief Counsel, Langley Research Center)</li></ul><b>OMSF Technical Support:</b><ul><li>Charles W. Mathews (Deputy Associate Administrator, Office of Manned Space Flight)</li></ul><b>Observers:</b><ul><li><a href="/wiki/William_Anders" title="William Anders">William A. Anders</a> (Executive Secretary, National Aeronautics and Space Council; ex-astronaut);</li>
<li>Dr. Charles D. Harrington (Chairman, NASA Aerospace Safety Advisory Panel);</li>
<li>I. I. Pinkel (Director, Aerospace Safety Research and Data Institute, Lewis Research Center).</li></ul><b>Congressional Liaison:</b><ul><li>Gerald J. Mossinghoff (Office of Legislative Affairs, NASA Hqs.)</li></ul><b>Public Affairs Liaison:</b><ul><li>Brian Duff (Public Affairs Officer. Manned Spacecraft Center)</li></ul><table>
<tbody><tr><td></td></tr></tbody></table></dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Activities_and_report"><span id="Review_board_report"></span> Activities and report</span></h3>
<p>The board exhaustively investigated and analyzed the history of the manufacture and testing of the oxygen tank, and its installation and testing in the spacecraft up to the Apollo 13 launch, as documented in detailed records and logs. They visited and consulted with engineers at the contractor's sites and the Kennedy Space Center. Once a theory of the cause was developed, elements of it were tested, including on a test rig simulation in a vacuum chamber, with a damaged tank installed in the fuel cell bay. This test confirmed the theory when a similar explosion was created, which blew off the outer panel exactly as happened in the flight.
Cortright sent the final <i>Report of Apollo 13 Review Board</i> to Thomas Paine on June 15, 1970.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>The failure started in the service module's number 2 oxygen tank.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Damaged <a href="/wiki/Polytetrafluoroethylene" title="Polytetrafluoroethylene">Teflon</a> insulation on the wires to the stirring fan inside oxygen tank 2 allowed the wires to <a href="/wiki/Short_circuit" title="Short circuit">short-circuit</a> and ignite this insulation. The resulting fire rapidly increased pressure beyond its 1,000-pound-per-square-inch (6.9 MPa) limit and the tank dome failed, filling the fuel cell bay (Sector 4) with rapidly expanding gaseous oxygen and combustion products. It is also possible some combustion occurred of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mylar" title="Mylar">Mylar</a>/<a href="/wiki/Kapton" title="Kapton">Kapton</a> thermal insulation material used to line the oxygen shelf compartment in this bay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-report_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-report-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>The resulting pressure inside the compartment popped the bolts attaching the 13-foot (4.0 m) Sector 4 outer aluminum skin panel, which as it blew off probably caused minor damage to the nearby S-band antenna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Shock_(mechanics)" title="Shock (mechanics)">Mechanical shock</a> forced the oxygen valves closed on the number 1 and number 3 fuel cells, leaving them operating for only about three minutes on the oxygen in the feed lines. The shock also either partially ruptured a line from the number 1 oxygen tank, or caused its check or relief valve to leak, causing its contents to leak out into space over the next 130 minutes, entirely depleting the SM's oxygen supply.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-report_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-report-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>The board determined the oxygen tank failure was caused by an unlikely chain of events. Tanks storing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryogen" title="Cryogen">cryogens</a>, such as liquid oxygen and liquid hydrogen, require either venting, extremely good insulation, or both, in order to avoid excessive pressure buildup due to vaporization of the tanks' contents. The service module oxygen tanks were so well insulated that they could safely contain <a href="/wiki/Supercritical_fluid" title="Supercritical fluid">supercritical</a> hydrogen and oxygen for years. Each oxygen tank held several hundred pounds of oxygen, which was used for breathable air and the production of electricity and water. The construction of the tanks made internal inspection impossible.
</p><p>The tank contained several components relevant to the accident:
</p>
<ul><li>a quantity sensor;</li>
<li>a fan to stir the tank contents for more accurate quantity measurements;</li>
<li>a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heater" title="Heater">heater</a> to vaporize liquid oxygen as needed;</li>
<li>a <a href="/wiki/Thermostat" title="Thermostat">thermostat</a> to protect the heater;</li>
<li>a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperature_sensor" title="Temperature sensor">temperature sensor</a>;</li>
<li>fill and drain valves and piping.</li></ul>
<p>The heater and protection thermostats were originally designed for the command module's 28-volt DC bus. The specifications for the heater and thermostat were later changed to allow a 65-volt ground supply, in order to pressurize the tanks more rapidly. <a href="/wiki/Beechcraft" title="Beechcraft">Beechcraft</a>, the tank subcontractor, did not upgrade the thermostat to handle the higher voltage.
</p><p>The oxygen shelf carrying the oxygen tanks was originally installed in the Apollo 10 service module, but was removed to fix a potential <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_interference" title="Electromagnetic interference">electromagnetic interference</a> problem. During removal, the shelf was accidentally dropped about 2 inches (5 cm) because a retaining bolt had not been removed. The tank appeared to be undamaged, but a loosely fitting filling tube was apparently damaged, and photographs suggested that the close-out cap on the top of the tank may have hit the fuel cell shelf. The report of the Apollo 13 review board considers the probability of tank damage during this incident to be "rather low."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-report_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-report-17">[17]</a></sup>
After the tank was filled for ground testing, it could not be emptied through the normal drain line. To avoid delaying the mission by replacing the tank, the heater was connected to 65-volt ground power to boil off the oxygen. Lovell signed off on this procedure. It should have taken a few days at the thermostatic opening temperature of 27 °C (81 °F). When the thermostat opened, the 65-volt supply fused its contacts closed and the heater remained powered. The board confirmed by testing that the thermostats welded themselves closed under the higher voltage.
This raised the temperature of the heater to an estimated 540 °C (1,000 °F). A <a href="/wiki/Chart_recorder" title="Chart recorder">chart recorder</a> on the heater current showed that the heater was not cycling on and off, as it should have been if the thermostat was functioning correctly, but no one noticed it at the time. Because the temperature sensor was not designed to read higher than the 27 °C (81 °F) thermostat opening temperature, the monitoring equipment did not register the true temperature inside the tank.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lostMoon_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-lostMoon-33">[33]</a></sup> The gas boiled off in hours rather than days.
</p><p>The sustained high temperatures melted the <a href="/wiki/Polytetrafluoroethylene" title="Polytetrafluoroethylene">Teflon</a> insulation on the fan power supply wires and left them exposed. When the tank was refilled with oxygen, it became a bomb waiting to go off. During the "cryo stir" procedure, fan power passed through the bare wires which apparently shorted, producing sparks and igniting the Teflon. This in turn boiled liquid oxygen faster than the tank vent could remove it.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo13_tank2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="582" data-file-width="626" decoding="async" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Apollo13_tank2.jpg/220px-Apollo13_tank2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Apollo13_tank2.jpg/330px-Apollo13_tank2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Apollo13_tank2.jpg/440px-Apollo13_tank2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo13_tank2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Apollo 13 details of oxygen tank number 2 and the heater and thermostat unit</div></div></div>
<p>In June 1970, the Cortright Report<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-report_17-4"><a href="#cite_note-report-17">[17]</a></sup> provided an in-depth analysis of the mission in an extremely detailed five-chapter report with eight appendices. It included a copy of established NASA procedures for alleviating high pressure in a cryogenic oxygen tank, to include:
</p>
<ul><li>Turning the four tank heaters and fans off;</li>
<li>Pulling the two heater circuit breakers to open to remove the energy source;</li>
<li>Performing a 2-minute purge, or directly opening the O<sub>2</sub> valve.</li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TankRuptureParametersGraph_-_Apollo13_CortrightReport_pg_B-47_(pg_A-209_inlay).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1749" data-file-width="2397" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/TankRuptureParametersGraph_-_Apollo13_CortrightReport_pg_B-47_%28pg_A-209_inlay%29.png/220px-TankRuptureParametersGraph_-_Apollo13_CortrightReport_pg_B-47_%28pg_A-209_inlay%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/TankRuptureParametersGraph_-_Apollo13_CortrightReport_pg_B-47_%28pg_A-209_inlay%29.png/330px-TankRuptureParametersGraph_-_Apollo13_CortrightReport_pg_B-47_%28pg_A-209_inlay%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/TankRuptureParametersGraph_-_Apollo13_CortrightReport_pg_B-47_%28pg_A-209_inlay%29.png/440px-TankRuptureParametersGraph_-_Apollo13_CortrightReport_pg_B-47_%28pg_A-209_inlay%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TankRuptureParametersGraph_-_Apollo13_CortrightReport_pg_B-47_(pg_A-209_inlay).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Telemetered parameters of the oxygen tank rupture incident, with inset image of pressure relief valve</div></div></div>
<p>This procedure was designed to prevent hardware failure so that the lunar landing mission could be continued. The <i>Mission Operations Report Apollo 13</i> recounts how the master caution and warning alarm had been turned off for a previous low-pressure reading on hydrogen tank 2, and so it did not trigger to call attention to the high oxygen pressure reading.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Oxygen tank 2 was not the only pressure vessel that failed during this mission. Prior to the accident, the crew had moved the scheduled entry into the lunar module forward by three hours. This was done to get an earlier look at the pressure reading of the supercritical helium (SHe) tank in the LM descent stage, which had been suspect since before launch. After the abort decision, the helium pressure continued to rise and Mission Control predicted the time that the burst disc would rupture. The helium tank <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burst_disc" title="Burst disc">burst disc</a> ruptured at 108:54, after the lunar flyby. The expulsion reversed the direction of the passive thermal control (PTC) roll (nicknamed the "barbecue roll").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>While the investigation board did recreate the oxygen tank failure, it did not report on any experiments that would show how effective the Cryogenic Malfunctions Procedures were to prevent the system failure by de-energizing the electrical heater and fan circuits.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Corrective_actions">Corrective actions</span></h3>
<p>The oxygen tank was redesigned, with the thermostats upgraded to handle the proper voltage. The heaters were retained since they were necessary to maintain oxygen pressure. The stirring fans, with their unsealed motors, were removed, which meant the oxygen quantity gauge was no longer accurate. This required adding a third tank so that no tank would go below half full.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gatland2_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gatland2-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>All electrical wiring in the power system bay was sheathed in stainless steel, and the oxygen quantity probes were changed from aluminum to stainless steel. The fuel cell oxygen supply valves were redesigned to isolate the Teflon-coated wiring from the oxygen. The spacecraft and Mission Control monitoring systems were modified to give more immediate and visible warnings of anomalies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gatland2_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gatland2-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_notes">Mission notes</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:President_Richard_Nixon_speaks_before_awarding_the_Apollo_13_astronauts_the_Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2556" data-file-width="3857" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/President_Richard_Nixon_speaks_before_awarding_the_Apollo_13_astronauts_the_Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom.jpg/220px-President_Richard_Nixon_speaks_before_awarding_the_Apollo_13_astronauts_the_Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/President_Richard_Nixon_speaks_before_awarding_the_Apollo_13_astronauts_the_Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom.jpg/330px-President_Richard_Nixon_speaks_before_awarding_the_Apollo_13_astronauts_the_Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/President_Richard_Nixon_speaks_before_awarding_the_Apollo_13_astronauts_the_Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom.jpg/440px-President_Richard_Nixon_speaks_before_awarding_the_Apollo_13_astronauts_the_Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:President_Richard_Nixon_speaks_before_awarding_the_Apollo_13_astronauts_the_Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>President <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> speaks before awarding the Apollo 13 astronauts the <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom" title="Presidential Medal of Freedom">Presidential Medal of Freedom</a></div></div></div>
<p>Because Apollo 13 followed the <a href="/wiki/Free-return_trajectory" title="Free-return trajectory">free-return trajectory</a>, its altitude over the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Far_side_(Moon)" title="Far side (Moon)">lunar far side</a> was approximately 100 km (60 mi) greater than the orbital altitude on the remaining Apollo lunar missions. The Moon was almost at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apogee" title="Apogee">apogee</a> during the mission (as it also was during the flights of Apollo 10 and Apollo 15), which also increased the distance from the Earth. The combination of the two effects ensures that Apollo 13 holds the absolute altitude record for a manned spacecraft, reaching a distance of 400,171 kilometers (248,655 mi) from Earth on 7:21 pm EST, April 14, 1970.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Apollo/Skylab_A7L" title="Apollo/Skylab A7L">A7L</a> spacesuit intended to be worn on the lunar surface by Lovell would have been the first to feature red bands on the arms, legs, lunar EVA helmet assembly, and the life-support backpack. This came about because Mission Control personnel watching the video feeds of Apollos 11 and 12 had trouble distinguishing the astronauts while both had their helmet sunshades down. The red bands were used for the remaining Apollo flights, the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_program" title="Space Shuttle program">Space Shuttle program</a>, and in the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a>.
</p><p>The Apollo 13 mission was called a "successful failure" by Lovell,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> because of the successful safe return of the astronauts, but the failed lunar landing. It has also been termed "NASA's finest hour."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walt_Disney's_Honorary_Oscars_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walt_Disney's_Honorary_Oscars-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>President Nixon awarded the <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom" title="Presidential Medal of Freedom">Presidential Medal of Freedom</a> to the crew and the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_13_Mission_Operations_Team" title="Apollo 13 Mission Operations Team">Apollo 13 Mission Operations Team</a> for their actions during the mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Cold Cathode Gauge Experiment (CCGE) which was part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apollo_Lunar_Surface_Experiment_Package" title="Apollo Lunar Surface Experiment Package">ALSEP</a> on Apollo 13 was never flown again. It was a version of the Cold Cathode Ion Gauge (CCIG) which featured on Apollo 12, Apollo 14, and <a href="/wiki/Apollo_15" title="Apollo 15">Apollo 15</a>. The CCGE was designed as a standalone version of the CCIG. On other missions, the CCIG was connected as part of the Suprathermal Ion Detector (SIDE). Because of the aborted landing, this experiment was never deployed. Other experiments included on Apollo 13's ALSEP included the Heat Flow Experiment (HFE), the Passive Seismic Experiment (PSE), and the Charged Particle Lunar Environment Experiment (CPLEE).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plaque_and_insignia">Plaque and insignia</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:APOLLO_13_LUNAR_PLAQUE_replica.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/APOLLO_13_LUNAR_PLAQUE_replica.jpg/220px-APOLLO_13_LUNAR_PLAQUE_replica.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/APOLLO_13_LUNAR_PLAQUE_replica.jpg/330px-APOLLO_13_LUNAR_PLAQUE_replica.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/APOLLO_13_LUNAR_PLAQUE_replica.jpg/440px-APOLLO_13_LUNAR_PLAQUE_replica.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:APOLLO_13_LUNAR_PLAQUE_replica.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Replica of the plaque with Swigert's name that was to replace the one attached to <i>Aquarius</i> that had Mattingly's name</div></div></div>
<p>The original <a href="/wiki/Lunar_plaque" title="Lunar plaque">lunar plaque</a> affixed to the front landing leg of <i>Aquarius</i> bore Mattingly's name, so a replacement plaque with Swigert's name was carried in the cabin, for Lovell to place over the other after he descended the ladder. He kept the plaque as a souvenir. In his book <i><a href="/wiki/Lost_Moon" title="Lost Moon">Lost Moon</a></i> (later renamed <i>Apollo 13</i>), Lovell stated that, apart from the plaque and a couple of other pieces, the only other memento he possesses is a letter from <a href="/wiki/Charles_Lindbergh" title="Charles Lindbergh">Charles Lindbergh</a>.
</p><p>The Apollo 13 crew patch featured three flying horses as Apollo's "chariot" across space. Given Lovell's Navy background, the logo also included the mottoes "<i>Ex Luna, scientia</i>" ("From the Moon, knowledge"), borrowed from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Naval_Academy" title="U.S. Naval Academy">U.S. Naval Academy</a>'s motto, "<i>Ex scientia tridens</i>" ("From knowledge, sea power"). The mission number appeared in <a href="/wiki/Roman_numerals" title="Roman numerals">Roman numerals</a> as Apollo XIII. The patch did not have to be modified after Mattingly's replacement since it is one of only two Apollo mission insignia—the other being Apollo 11—not to include the names of the crew. It was designed by artist <a href="/wiki/Lumen_Martin_Winter" title="Lumen Martin Winter">Lumen Martin Winter</a>, who based it on a mural he had done for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_St._Regis_Hotel" title="The St. Regis Hotel">The St. Regis Hotel</a> in New York City.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> The mural was later purchased by actor <a href="/wiki/Tom_Hanks" title="Tom Hanks">Tom Hanks</a>, who portrayed Lovell in the movie <i><a href="/wiki/Apollo_13_(film)" title="Apollo 13 (film)">Apollo 13</a></i>, and now is on the wall of a restaurant near Chicago owned by Lovell's son.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Successful_experiments">Successful experiments</span></h3>
<p>Despite Apollo 13's failure to land on the Moon, several experiments were conducted successfully because they were initiated before or conducted independently of the oxygen tank explosion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Several experiments to study electrical phenomena were conducted prior to and during the launch of Apollo 13. This information was used to better understand hazards of launching in less than ideal weather conditions.</li>
<li>Eleven photographs of Earth were taken at precisely recorded times, to study the feasibility of using geosynchronous satellites to study cloud height.</li>
<li>Apollo 13's <a href="/wiki/S-IVB" title="S-IVB">S-IVB</a> third stage was the first to be purposely crashed into the lunar surface, as an <i>active seismic</i> experiment which measured its impact with a seismometer left on the lunar surface by the crew of Apollo 12. (The S-IVBs from the previous four lunar missions were sent into solar orbit by ground control after use.)</li></ul>
<h3><span id=".22Towing_fees.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='"Towing_fees"'>"Towing fees"</span></h3>
<p>As a joke following Apollo 13's successful splashdown, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grumman_Aerospace_Corporation" title="Grumman Aerospace Corporation">Grumman Aerospace Corporation</a> pilot Sam Greenberg (who had helped with the strategy for re-routing power from the LM to the crippled CM) issued a tongue-in-cheek invoice for $400,540.05 to <a href="/wiki/Rockwell_International" title="Rockwell International">North American Rockwell</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pratt_and_Whitney" title="Pratt and Whitney">Pratt and Whitney</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beech_Aircraft" title="Beech Aircraft">Beech Aircraft</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> prime and subcontractors for the CSM, for "towing" the crippled ship most of the way to the Moon and back. The figure was based on an estimated 400,001 miles (643,739 km) at $1.00 per mile, plus $4.00 for the first mile. An extra $536.05 was included for battery charging, oxygen, and an "additional guest in room" (Swigert). A 20% "commercial discount," as well as a further 2% discount if North American were to pay in cash, reduced the total to $312,421.24.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> North American declined payment, noting that it had ferried three previous Grumman LMs to the Moon (Apollo 10, Apollo 11 and Apollo 12) with no such reciprocal charges.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Spacecraft_location">Spacecraft location</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo13_CommandModule_Kansas.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Apollo13_CommandModule_Kansas.JPG/220px-Apollo13_CommandModule_Kansas.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Apollo13_CommandModule_Kansas.JPG/330px-Apollo13_CommandModule_Kansas.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Apollo13_CommandModule_Kansas.JPG/440px-Apollo13_CommandModule_Kansas.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo13_CommandModule_Kansas.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Apollo 13 command module on display at the <a href="/wiki/Cosmosphere" title="Cosmosphere">Cosmosphere</a> in Hutchinson, Kansas</div></div></div>
<p>The command module shell was formerly at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_de_l%27air_et_de_l%27espace" title="Musée de l'air et de l'espace">Musée de l'air et de l'espace</a>, in Paris. The interior components were removed during the investigation of the accident and reassembled into <a href="/wiki/Boilerplate_(spaceflight)#Apollo_boilerplates" title="Boilerplate (spaceflight)">boilerplate</a> BP-1102A, the water egress training module; and were subsequently on display at the <a href="/wiki/Kentucky_Science_Center" title="Kentucky Science Center">Museum of Natural History and Science</a> in <a href="/wiki/Louisville,_Kentucky" title="Louisville, Kentucky">Louisville, Kentucky</a>, until 2000. The command module and the internal components were reassembled, and <i>Odyssey</i> is currently on display at the <a href="/wiki/Cosmosphere" title="Cosmosphere">Cosmosphere</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hutchinson,_Kansas" title="Hutchinson, Kansas">Hutchinson, Kansas</a>.
</p><p>The lunar module burned up in Earth's atmosphere on April 17, 1970, having been targeted to enter over the Pacific Ocean to reduce the possibility of contamination from a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Systems_Nuclear_Auxiliary_Power_Program" title="Systems Nuclear Auxiliary Power Program">SNAP 27</a> <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator" title="Radioisotope thermoelectric generator">radioisotope thermoelectric generator</a> (RTG) on board. Intended to power the mission's <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Lunar_Surface_Experiments_Package" title="Apollo Lunar Surface Experiments Package">ALSEP</a>, the RTG survived reentry (as designed) and landed in the <a href="/wiki/Tonga_Trench" title="Tonga Trench">Tonga Trench</a>. While it will remain radioactive for several thousand years, it does not appear to be releasing any of its 3.9 kg of radioactive <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-238" title="Plutonium-238">plutonium-238</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Lovell's lunar space suit helmet, one of his gloves, and the plaque that had been intended to be left on the Moon are on exhibit at the <a href="/wiki/Adler_Planetarium" title="Adler Planetarium">Adler Planetarium</a> in Chicago, Illinois.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Apollo 13 <a href="/wiki/S-IVB" title="S-IVB">S-IVB</a> with its Instrument Unit was guided to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_man-made_objects_on_the_Moon" title="List of man-made objects on the Moon">crash onto the lunar surface</a> on April 14, providing a signal for the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_12_Passive_Seismic_Experiment" title="Apollo 12 Passive Seismic Experiment">Apollo 12 Passive Seismic Experiment</a>.
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="max-width: 849px;_width: 849px;">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 275px"><div style="width: 275px">

</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 275px"><div style="width: 275px">
